      <p>
       The
       <b>
        Miami metropolitan area
       </b>
       , also known as the
       <b>
        Greater Miami Area
       </b>
       or
       <b>
        South Florida
       </b>
       , is the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_metropolitan_areas_by_population" title="List of metropolitan areas by population">
        73rd largest
       </a>
       metropolitan area in the world and the eighth-largest
       <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_area" title="Metropolitan area">
        metropolitan area
       </a>
       in the United States. It is entirely located in the southern portion of the U.S. state of
       <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">
        Florida
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With 6,066,387 inhabitants as of 2016, the Miami metropolitan area is the most populous in Florida and
       <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_United_States#Largest_metropolitan_areas,_2016" title="Southeastern United States">
        second largest in the southeastern United States
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The metropolitan area is defined by the
       <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Management_and_Budget" title="Office of Management and Budget">
        Office of Management and Budget
       </a>
       as the
       <b>
        Miami–Fort Lauderdale–West Palm Beach, FL
       </b>
       (MSA), consisting of
       <a href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_County,_Florida" title="Miami-Dade County, Florida">
        Miami-Dade
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Broward_County,_Florida" title="Broward County, Florida">
        Broward
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Palm_Beach_County,_Florida" title="Palm Beach County, Florida">
        Palm Beach
       </a>
       counties, a
       <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_statistical_area" title="Metropolitan statistical area">
        metropolitan statistical area
       </a>
       used for statistical purposes by the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">
        United States Census Bureau
       </a>
       and other agencies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its land area is 6,137 sq. mi (15,890 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_County,_Florida" title="Miami-Dade County, Florida">
        Miami-Dade
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Broward_County,_Florida" title="Broward County, Florida">
        Broward
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Palm_Beach_County,_Florida" title="Palm Beach County, Florida">
        Palm Beach
       </a>
       counties are the first, second, and third most populous
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_counties_in_Florida" title="List of counties in Florida">
        counties in Florida
       </a>
       , and Miami-Dade, with 2,693,117 people in 2014, is the seventh most populous county in the United States. The three counties together have principal cities including
       <a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">
        Miami
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fort_Lauderdale,_Florida" title="Fort Lauderdale, Florida">
        Fort Lauderdale
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pompano_Beach,_Florida" title="Pompano Beach, Florida">
        Pompano Beach
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/West_Palm_Beach,_Florida" title="West Palm Beach, Florida">
        West Palm Beach
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Boca_Raton,_Florida" title="Boca Raton, Florida">
        Boca Raton
       </a>
       . Besides its association with the
       <a href="/wiki/South_Florida" title="South Florida">
        South Florida
       </a>
       region, is also partially synonymous with an area known collectively as the
       <a href="/wiki/Gold_Coast_(Florida)" title="Gold Coast (Florida)">
        "Gold Coast"
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The Census Bureau also defines a wider region based on commuting patterns, the
       <b>
        Miami–Fort Lauderdale–Port St. Lucie, FL Combined Statistical Area
       </b>
       (CSA), also known as the Greater Miami Area, with an estimated population of 6,723,472 in 2016.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This includes the four additional counties of
       <a href="/wiki/Martin_County,_Florida" title="Martin County, Florida">
        Martin
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/St._Lucie_County,_Florida" title="St. Lucie County, Florida">
        St. Lucie
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_River_County,_Florida" title="Indian River County, Florida">
        Indian River
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Okeechobee_County,_Florida" title="Okeechobee County, Florida">
        Okeechobee
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Because the population of South Florida is largely confined to a strip of land between the Atlantic Ocean and the
       <a href="/wiki/Everglades" title="Everglades">
        Everglades
       </a>
       , the Miami
       <a href="/wiki/Urban_area#United_States" title="Urban area">
        urbanized area
       </a>
       (that is, the area of contiguous urban development) is about 100 miles (160 km) long (north to south), but never more than 20 miles (32 km) wide, and in some areas only 5 miles (8.0 km) wide (east to west). The Miami metropolitan statistical area is longer than any other urbanized area in the United States except for the
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_metropolitan_area" title="New York metropolitan area">
        New York metropolitan area
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was the eighth most densely populated urbanized area in the United States in the 2000 census.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As of the 2000 census, the urbanized area had a land area of 1,116 square miles (2,890 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ), with a population of 4,919,036, for a population density of 4,407.4 per square mile (1,701.7 per square kilometer). Miami and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hialeah" title="Hialeah">
        Hialeah
       </a>
       (the second largest city in the metropolitan area) had population densities of more than 10,000 per square mile (more than 3,800 per square kilometer).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Miami Urbanized Area was the fourth largest urbanized area in the United States in the 2010 census.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Miami metropolitan area also includes several
       <a href="/wiki/Urban_area#United_States" title="Urban area">
        urban clusters
       </a>
       (UCs) as of the 2000 Census which are not part of the Miami Urbanized Area. These are the
       <a href="/wiki/Belle_Glade,_Florida" title="Belle Glade, Florida">
        Belle Glade
       </a>
       UC, population 24,218, area 20,717,433 square meters and population density of 3027.6 per square mile;
       <a href="/wiki/Key_Biscayne,_Florida" title="Key Biscayne, Florida">
        Key Biscayne
       </a>
       UC, population 10,513, area 4,924,214 square meters and population density of 5529.5 per square mile;
       <a href="/wiki/Redland,_Florida" title="Redland, Florida">
        Redland
       </a>
       UC, population 3,936, area 10,586,212 square meters and population density of 963.0 per square mile; and
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter,_Florida" title="Jupiter, Florida">
        West Jupiter
       </a>
       UC, population 8,998, area 24,737,176 square meters and population density of 942.1 per square mile.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Metropolitan_divisions">
        Metropolitan divisions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miami_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Metropolitan divisions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Downtown_Miami_alt_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2831" data-file-width="4645" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Downtown_Miami_alt_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/280px-Downtown_Miami_alt_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Downtown_Miami_alt_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/420px-Downtown_Miami_alt_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Downtown_Miami_alt_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/560px-Downtown_Miami_alt_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg 2x" width="280"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Downtown_Miami_alt_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <b>
          Miami
         </b>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Miami metropolitan area consists of three distinct
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_metropolitan_area" title="United States metropolitan area">
        metropolitan divisions
       </a>
       , subdividing the region into three divisions according to the region's three counties:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_County" title="Miami-Dade County">
        Miami-Dade County
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broward_County" title="Broward County">
        Broward County
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palm_Beach_County" title="Palm Beach County">
        Palm Beach County
       </a>
       .
      </p>

        </a>

         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>

       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

        </a>

         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Miamimetroarea.jpg" title="Enlarge">

         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Miami_Beach,_Florida" title="Miami Beach, Florida">
          Miami Beach
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fort_Lauderdale_Skyline.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Fort_Lauderdale_Skyline.jpg/220px-Fort_Lauderdale_Skyline.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Fort_Lauderdale_Skyline.jpg/330px-Fort_Lauderdale_Skyline.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Fort_Lauderdale_Skyline.jpg/440px-Fort_Lauderdale_Skyline.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fort_Lauderdale_Skyline.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Fort_Lauderdale,_Florida" title="Fort Lauderdale, Florida">
          Fort Lauderdale
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mizner_Park.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Mizner_Park.jpg/220px-Mizner_Park.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Mizner_Park.jpg/330px-Mizner_Park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Mizner_Park.jpg/440px-Mizner_Park.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mizner_Park.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Boca_Raton,_Florida" title="Boca Raton, Florida">
          Boca Raton
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Miami area is a very diverse community with a large proportion of foreign-born residents, in large part due to its proximity to
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">
        Latin America
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">
        Caribbean
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Another large factor are residents who were former
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snowbird_(people)" title="Snowbird (people)">
        snowbirds
       </a>
       from the
       <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">
        Northeast
       </a>
       and, to a lesser extent, countries such as
       <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
        Canada
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Politically speaking, the region is heavily
       <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">
        Democratic
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Broward_County,_Florida" title="Broward County, Florida">
        Broward County
       </a>
       is the second most reliably Democratic county in the state,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broward_Power_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Broward_Power-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       behind only
       <a href="/wiki/Gadsden_County,_Florida" title="Gadsden County, Florida">
        Gadsden County
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Palm_Beach_County,_Florida" title="Palm Beach County, Florida">
        Palm Beach County
       </a>
       , like Broward, is largely Democratic as well, especially amongst its Jewish community,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while the rest of Florida tends to follow
       <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_the_Southern_United_States" title="Politics of the Southern United States">
        Southern politics
       </a>
       and vote more
       <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">
        Republican
       </a>
       , with the exception of certain parts of Florida where Southern culture is not as influential. With a majority Hispanic population in
       <a href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_County,_Florida" title="Miami-Dade County, Florida">
        Miami-Dade
       </a>
       , Republican votes are mainly by older generations of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuban_American" title="Cuban American">
        Cuban Americans
       </a>
       most of whom had fled to the United States to escape the Communist reign of
       <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">
        Fidel Castro
       </a>
       , but Miami-Dade County still remains very Democratic when compared with most of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florida_counties" title="Florida counties">
        Florida's other counties
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFSS_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SFSS-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 2016 presidential election, 62.3% of voters in the Miami metropolitan area voted Democratic. This was the 6th highest of any metro area in the United States.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As of the 2005 American Community Survey,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       5,334,685 people lived in the metropolis.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Miami area has a very large Jewish community; 10.2% of the population was Jewish in the
       <a href="/wiki/2000_United_States_Census" title="2000 United States Census">
        2000 Census
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       There is also a sizable
       <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_the_United_States" title="Islam in the United States">
        Muslim
       </a>
       community numbering at 70,000.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Population:
       </b>
       As of the 2010 U.S. Census, there were 5,564,635 people. 2.8 million (52%) were females and 2.6 million (48%) were males. The median age was 38.6 years. 24% of the population were under 18 years and 15% were 65 years and older. There were 2,097,626 households, and 1,378,108 families residing in the Miami metropolitan area.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Ethnicity:
       </b>
       <br/>
       The racial makeup of population of the Miami area [5,334,685] as of 2010:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_American" title="White American">
         White
        </a>
        : 70.3% [3,914,239]
        <ul>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/White_Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="White Hispanic and Latino Americans">
           White Hispanic
          </a>
          : 35.2% [1,976,300]
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-Hispanic_Whites" title="Non-Hispanic Whites">
           White Non-Hispanic
          </a>
          : 34.8% [1,937,939]
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">
         Black or African American
        </a>
        (many from the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Indian_American" title="West Indian American">
         Caribbean
        </a>
        ): 21% [1,075,174]
       </li>
       <li>
        Native American: 0.3% [16,108]
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_American" title="Asian American">
         Asian
        </a>
        : 2.3% [125,564]; (0.7% Indian, 0.5% Chinese, 0.3% Filipino, 0.2% Vietnamese, 0.1% Korean, 0.1% Japanese, 0.4% Other Asian)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Islander_American" title="Pacific Islander American">
         Pacific Islander
        </a>
        : 0.0% [2,356]
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Race_and_ethnicity_in_the_United_States_Census#Race" title="Race and ethnicity in the United States Census">
         Other races
        </a>
        : 3.5% [197,183]
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiracial_American" title="Multiracial American">
         Two or more races
        </a>
        : 2.5% [140,000]
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">
         Hispanic or Latino
        </a>
        (of any race) were 41.6% [2,312,929] of the population
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Language_and_national_origin">
        Language and national origin
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miami_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Language and national origin">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <b>
        National origin and language:
       </b>
       Of the people living in the Miami metro area in 2005, 63% were born in the United States (including 30% who were born in Florida) and 37% were
       <a href="/wiki/Foreign_born" title="Foreign born">
        foreign born
       </a>
       . Among people at least five years old living in the region in 2005, 52% spoke English at home while 48% spoke some other language at home. Of those speaking a language other than English at home, 78% spoke Spanish and 22% spoke some other language (mainly
       <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Creole" title="Haitian Creole">
        Haitian Creole
       </a>
       , but also French, German,
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
        Hebrew
       </a>
       , Italian,
       <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Portuguese" title="Brazilian Portuguese">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       , Russian or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yiddish_language" title="Yiddish language">
        Yiddish
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Geographic mobility:
       </b>
       In 2005, 83% of the people at least one year old living in the Miami metro area were living in the same residence one year earlier; 12% had moved during the past year from another residence in the same county, 2% from another county in the same state, 2% from another state, and 1% from abroad.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Households and families:
       </b>
       There were 2,338,450 households, The average household size was 2.6 people. Families made up 65% of the households in the Miami area. This figure includes both married-couple families (45%) and other families (20%). Nonfamily households made up 35% of all households in Miami. Most of the nonfamily households were people living alone, but some consisted of people living in households in which no one was related to the householder.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Miami_dialect">
        Miami dialect
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miami_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Miami dialect">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Miami_accent" title="Miami accent">
        Miami accent
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In and around
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_County" title="Miami-Dade County">
        Miami-Dade County
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broward_County" title="Broward County">
        Broward County
       </a>
       , a unique dialect, commonly called the "Miami dialect", is widely spoken. The dialect developed among second- or third-generation
       <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">
        Hispanics
       </a>
       , including
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuban-American" title="Cuban-American">
        Cuban-Americans
       </a>
       , whose first language was English (though some
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-Hispanic_white" title="Non-Hispanic white">
        non-Hispanic white
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Black_people" title="Black people">
        black
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_in_the_US" title="Race in the US">
        other races
       </a>
       who were born and raised in Miami-Dade tend to adopt it as well.)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is based on a fairly
       <a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">
        standard American accent
       </a>
       but with some changes very similar to dialects in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_States" title="Mid-Atlantic States">
        Mid-Atlantic
       </a>
       (especially the
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_English" title="New York City English">
        New York area dialect
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_English#North_Jersey_English" title="New Jersey English">
        Northern New Jersey English
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_Latino_English" title="New York Latino English">
        New York Latino English
       </a>
       .) Unlike
       <a href="/wiki/Piedmont_region_of_Virginia" title="Piedmont region of Virginia">
        Virginia Piedmont
       </a>
       , Coastal Southern American, and Northeast American dialects and Florida Cracker dialect (see section below), "Miami accent" is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhotic_and_non-rhotic_accents" title="Rhotic and non-rhotic accents">
        rhotic
       </a>
       ; it also incorporates a rhythm and pronunciation heavily influenced by Spanish (wherein rhythm is
       <a href="/wiki/Isochrony#syllable_timing" title="Isochrony">
        syllable-timed
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, this is a native dialect of English, not learner English or
       <a href="/wiki/Interlanguage" title="Interlanguage">
        interlanguage
       </a>
       ; it is possible to differentiate this variety from an
       <a href="/wiki/Interlanguage" title="Interlanguage">
        interlanguage
       </a>
       spoken by second-language speakers in that "Miami accent" does
       <i>
        not
       </i>
       generally display the following features: there is no
       <a href="/wiki/Epenthesis" title="Epenthesis">
        addition
       </a>
       of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɛ/
       </span>
       before initial consonant clusters with
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /s/
       </span>
       , speakers do not confuse of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /dʒ/
       </span>
       with
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /j/
       </span>
       , (e.g.,
       <i>
        Yale
       </i>
       with
       <i>
        jail
       </i>
       ), and /r/ and /rr/ are pronounced as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_approximant" title="Alveolar approximant">
        alveolar approximant [
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ɹ
        </span>
        ]
       </a>
       instead of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_tap" title="Alveolar tap">
        alveolar tap
       </a>
       [
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɾ
       </span>
       ] or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
        alveolar trill
       </a>
       [r] in Spanish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Education">
        Education
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miami_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Education">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <b>
        Education:
       </b>
       In 2005, 83% of people 25 years and over had at least graduated from high school and 30% had a bachelor's degree or higher. Among people 16 to 19 years old, 7% were dropouts; they were not enrolled in school and had not graduated from high school. The total school enrollment in Miami Metro Area was 1.4 million in 2005. Nursery school and kindergarten enrollment was 170,000 and elementary or high school enrollment was 879,000 children. College or graduate school enrollment was 354,000.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Occupation.2C_Income.2C_and_Industries">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Occupation,_Income,_and_Industries">
        Occupation, Income, and Industries
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>

       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Miami_skyline_northern_Brickell_20100206.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2300" data-file-width="3070" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Miami_skyline_northern_Brickell_20100206.jpg/220px-Miami_skyline_northern_Brickell_20100206.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Miami_skyline_northern_Brickell_20100206.jpg/330px-Miami_skyline_northern_Brickell_20100206.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Miami_skyline_northern_Brickell_20100206.jpg/440px-Miami_skyline_northern_Brickell_20100206.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Miami_skyline_northern_Brickell_20100206.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The urban neighborhood of
         <a href="/wiki/Brickell" title="Brickell">
          Brickell
         </a>
         in Downtown Miami contains the largest concentration of international banks in the U.S.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <b>
        Occupations and Type of Employer:
       </b>
       Among the most common occupations were: 32% were management, professional, and related occupations, 30% were sales and office occupations, 18% were service occupations, 11% were construction, extraction, maintenance and repair occupations, and 9% were production, transportation, and material moving occupations. 81% of the people employed were Private wage and salary workers; 12% were Federal, state, or local government workers; and 7% were Self-employed in own not incorporated business workers.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Income:
       </b>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Per_capita_income" title="Per capita income">
        median income
       </a>
       of households in the Miami area was $43,091. 78% of the households received earnings and 13% received retirement income other than Social Security. 30% of the households received Social Security. The average income from Social Security was $13. These income sources are not mutually exclusive; that is, some households received income from more than one source.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Industries:
       </b>
       In 2005, for the employed population 16 years and older, the leading industries in the Miami area were Educational services, health care and social assistance, which accounted for 18%, and Professional, scientific, and management, and administrative and waste management services, which accounted for 13% of the population.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Traveling to Work:
       </b>
       79% of Miami area workers drove to work alone in 2005, 10% carpooled, 4% took public transportation, and 4% used other means. The remaining 3% worked at home. Among those who commuted to work, it took them on average 28.5 minutes to get to work.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Poverty and Participation in Government Programs:
       </b>
       In 2005, 14% of people were in poverty. 19% of related children under 18 were below the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poverty_line" title="Poverty line">
        poverty level
       </a>
       , compared with 14% of people 65 years old and over. 11% of all families, and 26% of families with a female householder and no husband present had incomes below the poverty level.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Housing_characteristics_and_costs">
        Housing characteristics and costs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miami_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Housing characteristics and costs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Changes in
       <a href="/wiki/House_price_index" title="House price index">
        house prices
       </a>
       for the area are publicly tracked on a regular basis using the
       <a href="/wiki/Case%E2%80%93Shiller_index" title="Case–Shiller index">
        Case–Shiller index
       </a>
       ; the statistic is published by
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_%26_Poor%27s" title="Standard &amp; Poor's">
        Standard &amp; Poor's
       </a>
       and is also a component of S&amp;P's 10-city
       <a href="/wiki/Composite_(finance)" title="Composite (finance)">
        composite index
       </a>
       of the value of the residential real estate market.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Housing characteristics:
       </b>
       As of 2005, the Miami area had a total of 2.3 million housing units, 13% of which were vacant. Of the total housing units, 52% were in single-unit structures, 45% were in multi-unit structures, and 3% were mobile homes. 25% of the housing units were built since 1990.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Occupied housing unit characteristics:
       </b>
       In 2005, the Miami area had 2.0 million occupied housing units – 1.3 million (66%) owner occupied and 688,000 (34%) renter occupied.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Housing costs:
       </b>
       In 2010, housing costs in the Miami area typically represented 40% of household income, compared to 34% nationwide.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Property tax increase:
       </b>
       In March 2009, Miami area lawmakers passed a 5–10% hike in property tax millage rates throughout the metropolitan area to fund the construction of new schools and to fund understaffed schools and educational institutions, resulting in an increase in residents' property tax bills beginning in the 2009 tax year.
      </p>

         </a>
        </div>
       </div>

      <p>
       In Florida, each county is also a
       <a href="/wiki/School_district" title="School district">
        school district
       </a>
       . Each district is headed by an elected
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/School_board" title="School board">
        school board
       </a>
       . A professional
       <a href="/wiki/Superintendent_(education)" title="Superintendent (education)">
        superintendent
       </a>
       manages the day-to-day operations of each district, who is appointed by and serves at the pleasure of the School Board.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_County_Public_Schools" title="Miami-Dade County Public Schools">
        Miami-Dade County Public School District
       </a>
       is currently the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_largest_school_districts_in_the_United_States_by_enrollment" title="List of the largest school districts in the United States by enrollment">
        4th-largest public school district
       </a>
       in the nation. The
       <a href="/wiki/School_District_of_Palm_Beach_County" title="School District of Palm Beach County">
        School District of Palm Beach County
       </a>
       is the 4th-largest in Florida and the 11th-largest in the United States.
       <a href="/wiki/Broward_County_Public_Schools" title="Broward County Public Schools">
        Broward County Public School District
       </a>
       is the 6th-largest in the United States.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some colleges and universities in Greater Miami include:
      </p>
      <ul>

       </li>

      <p>
       In 2005, 82% of people 25 years and over had at least graduated from high school and 28% had a
       <a href="/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degree" title="Bachelor's degree">
        bachelor's degree
       </a>
       or higher. Among people 16 to 19 years old, 7% were
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dropping_out" title="Dropping out">
        dropouts
       </a>
       ; they were not enrolled in school and had not graduated from high school. The total school enrollment in the Miami metro area was 1.4 million in 2005. Nursery school and
       <a href="/wiki/Kindergarten" title="Kindergarten">
        kindergarten
       </a>
       enrollment was 170,000 and elementary or high school enrollment was 879,000. College or graduate school enrollment was 354,000.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">
        Transportation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miami_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Transportation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tri-Rail_at_Delray_Beach_Station.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="2000" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Tri-Rail_at_Delray_Beach_Station.jpg/200px-Tri-Rail_at_Delray_Beach_Station.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Tri-Rail_at_Delray_Beach_Station.jpg/300px-Tri-Rail_at_Delray_Beach_Station.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Tri-Rail_at_Delray_Beach_Station.jpg/400px-Tri-Rail_at_Delray_Beach_Station.jpg 2x" width="200"/>

         </a>
         is Miami's commuter rail and connects to the three counties north to south
        </div>

      </div>

      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Transportation_in_South_Florida" title="Transportation in South Florida">

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Roads">
        Roads
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miami_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Roads">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Miami metropolitan area is served by five
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_highways" title="Interstate highways">
        interstate highways
       </a>
       operated by the
       <a href="/wiki/Florida_Department_of_Transportation" title="Florida Department of Transportation">
        Florida Department of Transportation
       </a>
       (FDOT) in conjunction with local agencies.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_95_(Florida)" title="Interstate 95 (Florida)">
        Interstate 95 (I-95)
       </a>
       runs north to south along the coast, ending just south of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downtown_Miami" title="Downtown Miami">
        Downtown Miami
       </a>
       at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Dixie_Highway" title="South Dixie Highway">
        South Dixie Highway
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_1_in_Florida" title="U.S. Route 1 in Florida">
        US 1
       </a>
       ).
       <a href="/wiki/Interstate_75_in_Florida" title="Interstate 75 in Florida">
        I-75
       </a>
       runs east to west, turning south in western Broward County and connecting suburban north Miami-Dade to
       <a href="/wiki/Naples,_Florida" title="Naples, Florida">
        Naples
       </a>
       on the
       <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Florida" title="Southwest Florida">
        Southwest Coast
       </a>
       via
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alligator_Alley" title="Alligator Alley">
        Alligator Alley
       </a>
       , which transverses the Florida
       <a href="/wiki/Everglades" title="Everglades">
        Everglades
       </a>
       before turning north.
       <a href="/wiki/Interstate_595_(Florida)" title="Interstate 595 (Florida)">
        I-595
       </a>
       connects the Broward coast and downtown
       <a href="/wiki/Fort_Lauderdale,_Florida" title="Fort Lauderdale, Florida">
        Fort Lauderdale
       </a>
       to I-75 and Alligator Alley. In Miami,
       <a href="/wiki/Interstate_195_(Florida)" title="Interstate 195 (Florida)">
        I-195
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_395_(Florida)" title="Interstate 395 (Florida)">
        I-395
       </a>
       relay the main I-95 route east to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biscayne_Boulevard" title="Biscayne Boulevard">
        Biscayne Boulevard
       </a>
       (US 1) and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miami_Beach" title="Miami Beach">
        Miami Beach
       </a>
       across
       <a href="/wiki/Biscayne_Bay" title="Biscayne Bay">
        Biscayne Bay
       </a>
       via the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julia_Tuttle_Causeway" title="Julia Tuttle Causeway">
        Julia Tuttle
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/MacArthur_Causeway" title="MacArthur Causeway">
        MacArthur
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Causeway" title="Causeway">
        causeways
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In greater Miami, the
       <a href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_Expressway_Authority" title="Miami-Dade Expressway Authority">
        Miami-Dade Expressway Authority
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Florida%27s_Turnpike_Enterprise" title="Florida's Turnpike Enterprise">
        Florida's Turnpike Enterprise
       </a>
       (FTE) maintain eight
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_Roads_in_Florida" title="State Roads in Florida">
        state
       </a>
       expressways in conjunction with FDOT. The
       <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_112" title="Florida State Road 112">
        Airport Expressway (SR 112)
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_836" title="Florida State Road 836">
        Dolphin Expressway (SR 836)
       </a>
       relay western Miami-Dade suburbs to the eastern urban coast at I-95, and to Miami Beach via I-195 and I-395 at the Airport and
       <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Interchange" title="Midtown Interchange">
        Midtown
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Interchange_(road)" title="Interchange (road)">
        interchanges
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_924" title="Florida State Road 924">
        Gratigny Parkway (SR 924)
       </a>
       connects northern Miami suburbs to the southern end of I-75. The
       <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_826" title="Florida State Road 826">
        Palmetto Expressway (SR 826)
       </a>
       is the primary
       <a href="/wiki/Ring_road" title="Ring road">
        beltway road
       </a>
       of urban Miami, relaying I-95 and
       <a href="/wiki/Florida%27s_Turnpike" title="Florida's Turnpike">
        Florida's Turnpike
       </a>
       (SR 91) at the
       <a href="/wiki/Golden_Glades_Interchange" title="Golden Glades Interchange">
        Golden Glades Interchange
       </a>
       near northeastern
       <a href="/wiki/North_Miami_Beach,_Florida" title="North Miami Beach, Florida">
        North Miami Beach
       </a>
       to the southern inland suburbs of
       <a href="/wiki/Kendall,_Florida" title="Kendall, Florida">
        Kendall
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pinecrest,_Florida" title="Pinecrest, Florida">
        Pinecrest
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_874" title="Florida State Road 874">
        Don Shula Expressway (SR 874)
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Homestead_Extension_of_Florida%27s_Turnpike" title="Homestead Extension of Florida's Turnpike">
        Homestead Extension of Florida's Turnpike
       </a>
       (SR 821) form the southernmost end of the beltway, connecting the Palmetto Expressway to the
       <a href="/wiki/Commuter_town" title="Commuter town">
        bedroom communities
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Homestead,_Florida" title="Homestead, Florida">
        Homestead
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Florida_City,_Florida" title="Florida City, Florida">
        Florida City
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_878" title="Florida State Road 878">
        Snapper Creek Expressway (SR 878)
       </a>
       relays the Don Shula Expressway to South Dixie Highway (US 1).
      </p>
      <p>
       The urban
       <a href="/wiki/Bypass_(road)" title="Bypass (road)">
        bypass
       </a>
       expressway in greater Fort Lauderdale is the
       <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_869" title="Florida State Road 869">
        Sawgrass Expressway (SR 869)
       </a>
       , connecting the northern Broward County coast at I-95 and
       <a href="/wiki/Deerfield_Beach,_Florida" title="Deerfield Beach, Florida">
        Deerfield Beach
       </a>
       to I-595 and I-75 at Alligator Alley in
       <a href="/wiki/Sunrise,_Florida" title="Sunrise, Florida">
        Sunrise
       </a>
       .
      </p>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Major_airports">
        Major airports
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miami_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Major airports">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The metropolitan area is served by three major commercial airports. These airports combine to make the fourth largest domestic origin and destination market in the United States, after New York City, Los Angeles, and Chicago.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Seaports">
        Seaports
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miami_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Seaports">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The metropolis also has four seaports, the largest and most important being the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_of_Miami" title="Port of Miami">
        Port of Miami
       </a>
       . Others in the area include
       <a href="/wiki/Port_Everglades" title="Port Everglades">
        Port Everglades
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Palm_Beach" title="Port of Palm Beach">
        Port of Palm Beach
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Miami_River_(Florida)" title="Miami River (Florida)">
        Miami River
       </a>
       Port. On August 21, 2012, PortMiami and the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers signed the Partnership Agreement (PPA) construction agreement that will allow the Deep Dredge project to go out for bid. The Deep Dredge will deepen the Port's existing channels to minus 50/52 feet to prepare for the Panama Canal expansion, now scheduled for completion in early 2015. PortMiami's deeper channel will provide ships with an economically efficient, reliable and safe navigational route into the Port. PortMiami will be the only U.S. Port south of Norfolk, Virginia to be at the minus 50 foot depth in sync with the opening of the expanded Canal. Deep Dredge is expected to create more than 30,000 direct, indirect, and induced jobs in Florida and allow the Port to meet its goal to double its cargo traffic over the next decade.
      </p>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg">
          <img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/>
         </a>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Public_transportation">
        Public transportation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miami_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Public transportation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Miami_Metro_Map_2017.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4600" data-file-width="5500" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Miami_Metro_Map_2017.png/220px-Miami_Metro_Map_2017.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Miami_Metro_Map_2017.png/330px-Miami_Metro_Map_2017.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Miami_Metro_Map_2017.png/440px-Miami_Metro_Map_2017.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Miami_Metro_Map_2017.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Schematic of 2018 rapid transit and passenger rail service in the Miami metropolitan area in. Tri-Rail Downtown Miami Link and Brightline are scheduled to be operational by the end of the first quarter.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_Transit" title="Miami-Dade Transit">
        Miami-Dade Transit
       </a>
       (MDT) is the largest public transit agency in Florida, operating
       <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">
        rapid transit
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/People_mover" title="People mover">
        people movers
       </a>
       , and an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intercity_bus" title="Intercity bus">
        intercity bus
       </a>
       system.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metrorail_(Miami)" title="Metrorail (Miami)">
        Metrorail
       </a>
       is Florida's only
       <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">
        rapid transit
       </a>
       , currently with 23 stations on a 24.4-mile (39.3 km) track. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downtown_Miami" title="Downtown Miami">
        Downtown Miami
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/People_mover" title="People mover">
        people mover
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Metromover" title="Metromover">
        Metromover
       </a>
       , operates 20 stations and three lines on a 4.4-mile (7.1 km) track through the Downtown
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neighborhoods_of_Miami" title="Neighborhoods of Miami">
        neighborhoods
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Omni_(Miami)" title="Omni (Miami)">
        Omni
       </a>
       , the central Downtown, and
       <a href="/wiki/Brickell" title="Brickell">
        Brickell
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_Transit#Metrobus" title="Miami-Dade Transit">
        Metrobus
       </a>
       serves the entirety of Miami-Dade County, also serving
       <a href="/wiki/Monroe_County,_Florida" title="Monroe County, Florida">
        Monroe County
       </a>
       as far south as
       <a href="/wiki/Marathon,_Florida" title="Marathon, Florida">
        Marathon
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Broward_County,_Florida" title="Broward County, Florida">
        Broward County
       </a>
       as far north as downtown Fort Lauderdale. In Broward County,
       <a href="/wiki/Broward_County_Transit" title="Broward County Transit">
        Broward County Transit
       </a>
       runs public buses, as does
       <a href="/wiki/Palm_Tran" title="Palm Tran">
        Palm Tran
       </a>
       in Palm Beach County. Additionally, the
       <a href="/wiki/South_Florida_Regional_Transportation_Authority" title="South Florida Regional Transportation Authority">
        South Florida Regional Transportation Authority
       </a>
       operates
       <a href="/wiki/Tri-Rail" title="Tri-Rail">
        Tri-Rail
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Commuter_rail" title="Commuter rail">
        commuter rail
       </a>
       train that connects the three of the primary cities of South Florida (Miami, Fort Lauderdale, and West Palm Beach), and most intermediate points.
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">
        Climate
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miami_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Climate">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Miami" title="Climate of Miami">
        Climate of Miami
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Biscayne_National_Park.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="198" data-file-width="300" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Biscayne_National_Park.jpg/220px-Biscayne_National_Park.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/Biscayne_National_Park.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Biscayne_National_Park.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A view of
         <a href="/wiki/Biscayne_National_Park" title="Biscayne National Park">
          Biscayne National Park
         </a>
         in Miami-Dade County
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       South Florida has a
       <a href="/wiki/Tropical_climate" title="Tropical climate">
        tropical climate
       </a>
       , the only major metropolitan area in the 48 contiguous states that falls under that category. More specifically, it generally has a
       <a href="/wiki/Tropical_monsoon_climate" title="Tropical monsoon climate">
        tropical monsoon climate
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">
        Köppen climate classification
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        Am
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The South Florida metropolis sees most of its rain in the summer (wet season) and is quite dry in winter (dry season). The wet season, which is hot and humid, lasts from May to October, when daily thunderstorms and passing weak tropical lows bring downpours during the late afternoon. The dry season often starts in late October and runs through late April. During the height of the dry season from February through April, south Florida is often very dry, and often brush fires and water restrictions are an issue. At times cold fronts can make it all the way down to south Florida and provide some modest rainfall in the dry season. The
       <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="Atlantic hurricane season">
        hurricane season
       </a>
       largely coincides with the wet season.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In addition to its sea-level elevation, coastal location and position just above the
       <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Cancer" title="Tropic of Cancer">
        Tropic of Cancer
       </a>
       , the area owes its warm, humid climate to the
       <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">
        Gulf Stream
       </a>
       , which moderates climate year-round. A typical summer day does not see temperatures below 75 °F (24 °C). Temperatures in the high 80s to low 90s (30–35 °C) accompanied by high humidity are often relieved by afternoon
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thunderstorms" title="Thunderstorms">
        thunderstorms
       </a>
       or a
       <a href="/wiki/Sea_breeze" title="Sea breeze">
        sea breeze
       </a>
       that develops off the Atlantic Ocean, which then allow lower temperatures, although conditions still remain very muggy. During winter, dry air often dominates as dew points are often very low. Average daily highs across South Florida in winter are around 75 – 77 F, though daily highs in the low and even mid-80's are not uncommon. Minimum temperatures during the winter season are generally in the mid 50s to around 60 °F (10–15 °C), occasionally dipping into the upper 40s (7–9 °C) and rarely below 40 °F (5 °C). On average coastal South Florida is frost free, although there can be a frost inland a few times each decade.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Atlantic_hurricane_seasons" title="List of Atlantic hurricane seasons">
        Hurricane season
       </a>
       officially runs from June 1 through November 30, although hurricanes can develop outside that period. The most likely time for South Florida to be hit is during the peak of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cape_Verde-type_hurricane" title="Cape Verde-type hurricane">
        Cape Verde
       </a>
       season, mid-August through the end of September.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Due to its location between two major bodies of water known for tropical activity, South Florida is also statistically the most likely major area to be struck by a hurricane in the world, trailed closely by
       <a href="/wiki/Nassau,_Bahamas" title="Nassau, Bahamas">
        Nassau, Bahamas
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">
        Havana
       </a>
       , Cuba. Many hurricanes have affected the metropolis, including
       <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Betsy" title="Hurricane Betsy">
        Betsy
       </a>
       in 1965,
       <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Andrew" title="Hurricane Andrew">
        Andrew
       </a>
       in 1992,
       <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Irene_(1999)" title="Hurricane Irene (1999)">
        Irene
       </a>
       in 1999, Hurricanes
       <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">
        Katrina
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Wilma" title="Hurricane Wilma">
        Wilma
       </a>
       in 2005, and
       <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Irma" title="Hurricane Irma">
        Irma
       </a>
       in 2017. In addition, a tropical depression in October 2000 passed over the city, causing record rainfall and flooding. Locally, the storm is credited as the
       <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Leslie_(2000)" title="Tropical Storm Leslie (2000)">
        No Name Storm of 2000
       </a>
       , though the depression went on to become Tropical Storm Leslie upon entering the Atlantic Ocean.
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">
        Sports
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miami_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Sports">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:American_Airlines_Arena,_Miami,_FL,_jjron_29.03.2012.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2102" data-file-width="3346" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/American_Airlines_Arena%2C_Miami%2C_FL%2C_jjron_29.03.2012.jpg/220px-American_Airlines_Arena%2C_Miami%2C_FL%2C_jjron_29.03.2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/American_Airlines_Arena%2C_Miami%2C_FL%2C_jjron_29.03.2012.jpg/330px-American_Airlines_Arena%2C_Miami%2C_FL%2C_jjron_29.03.2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/American_Airlines_Arena%2C_Miami%2C_FL%2C_jjron_29.03.2012.jpg/440px-American_Airlines_Arena%2C_Miami%2C_FL%2C_jjron_29.03.2012.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:American_Airlines_Arena,_Miami,_FL,_jjron_29.03.2012.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines_Arena" title="American Airlines Arena">
          American Airlines Arena
         </a>
         , home of the
         <a href="/wiki/Miami_Heat" title="Miami Heat">
          Miami Heat
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Miami" title="Sport in Miami">
        Sport in Miami
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Miami area is home to four major professional sports teams:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Miami_Dolphins" title="Miami Dolphins">
         Miami Dolphins
        </a>
        of the
        <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">
         National Football League
        </a>
        play at
        <a href="/wiki/Hard_Rock_Stadium" title="Hard Rock Stadium">
         Hard Rock Stadium
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Miami_Gardens,_Florida" title="Miami Gardens, Florida">
         Miami Gardens
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Miami_Heat" title="Miami Heat">
         Miami Heat
        </a>
        of the
        <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">
         National Basketball Association
        </a>
        play at
        <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines_Arena" title="American Airlines Arena">
         American Airlines Arena
        </a>
        in
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downtown_Miami" title="Downtown Miami">
         Downtown Miami
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Miami_Marlins" title="Miami Marlins">
         Miami Marlins
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">
         Major League Baseball
        </a>
        play at
        <a href="/wiki/Marlins_Park" title="Marlins Park">
         Marlins Park
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Little_Havana" title="Little Havana">
         Little Havana
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Florida_Panthers" title="Florida Panthers">
         Florida Panthers
        </a>
        of the
        <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">
         National Hockey League
        </a>
        play at
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BB%26T_Center_(Sunrise)" title="BB&amp;T Center (Sunrise)">
         BB&amp;T Center
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Sunrise,_Florida" title="Sunrise, Florida">
         Sunrise
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The Miami area is also host to minor league sports, college sports, and other sports:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_Lauderdale_Strikers_(2011)" title="Fort Lauderdale Strikers (2011)">
         Fort Lauderdale Strikers
        </a>
        of the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_American_Soccer_League_(2010)" title="North American Soccer League (2010)">
         North American Soccer League
        </a>
        play at
        <a href="/wiki/Lockhart_Stadium" title="Lockhart Stadium">
         Lockhart Stadium
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Fort_Lauderdale,_Florida" title="Fort Lauderdale, Florida">
         Fort Lauderdale
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Miami_Marlins" title="Miami Marlins">
         Miami Marlins
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Cardinals" title="St. Louis Cardinals">
         St. Louis Cardinals
        </a>
        conduct
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spring_Training" title="Spring Training">
         Spring Training
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Jupiter,_Florida" title="Jupiter, Florida">
         Jupiter
        </a>
        at
        <a href="/wiki/Roger_Dean_Stadium" title="Roger Dean Stadium">
         Roger Dean Stadium
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Orioles" title="Baltimore Orioles">
         Baltimore Orioles
        </a>
        conduct
        <a href="/wiki/Spring_training" title="Spring training">
         Spring training
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Fort_Lauderdale,_Florida" title="Fort Lauderdale, Florida">
         Fort Lauderdale
        </a>
        at
        <a href="/wiki/Fort_Lauderdale_Stadium" title="Fort Lauderdale Stadium">
         Fort Lauderdale Stadium
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The college sports teams of
        <a href="/wiki/Barry_University" title="Barry University">
         Barry University
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Florida_Atlantic_University" title="Florida Atlantic University">
         Florida Atlantic University
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Florida_International_University" title="Florida International University">
         Florida International University
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Nova_Southeastern_University" title="Nova Southeastern University">
         Nova Southeastern University
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/University_of_Miami" title="University of Miami">
         University of Miami
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Homestead-Miami_Speedway" title="Homestead-Miami Speedway">
         Homestead-Miami Speedway
        </a>
        oval has hosted
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASCAR_Cup_Series" title="NASCAR Cup Series">
         NASCAR Cup Series
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/IndyCar_Series" title="IndyCar Series">
         IndyCar Series
        </a>
        events. Also, the
        <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Grand_Prix_of_Miami_(disambiguation)" title="Grand Prix of Miami (disambiguation)">
         streets of Miami
        </a>
        have hosted several
        <a href="/wiki/Champ_Car" title="Champ Car">
         CART
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IMSA_GT" title="IMSA GT">
         IMSA GT
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/American_Le_Mans_Series" title="American Le Mans Series">
         American Le Mans Series
        </a>
        races. The
        <a href="/wiki/Palm_Beach_International_Raceway" title="Palm Beach International Raceway">
         Palm Beach International Raceway
        </a>
        is a minor road course.
       </li>
      </ul>

        </td>
        <td align="center">
         19,710
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/NBA_Finals" title="NBA Finals">
          NBA Finals
         </a>
         (3) — 2006, 2012, 2013
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

        </td>

        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dolphin_Stadium_baseball_diamond.jpg">

         </a>

         <a href="/wiki/Miami_Dolphins" title="Miami Dolphins">

         , and the
         <a href="/wiki/Miami_Hurricanes" title="Miami Hurricanes">
          Miami Hurricanes
         </a>
        </div>
